sexual violence</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Women_in_war" title="Women in war">Women in war</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/World_war" title="World war">World war</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Colonial_war" title="Colonial war">Colonial war</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Endemic_warfare" title="Endemic warfare">Endemic warfare</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left"><a href="/wiki/List_of_military_writers" title="List of military writers">Writers</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div href="/wiki/Template_talk:War" title="Template talk:War"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:War&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gorskii._Austrian_prisoners_of_war_in_Olonets_province.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Gorskii._Austrian_prisoners_of_war_in_Olonets_province.jpg/220px-Gorskii._Austrian_prisoners_of_war_in_Olonets_province.jpg" width="220" height="190" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Gorskii._Austrian_prisoners_of_war_in_Olonets_province.jpg/330px-Gorskii._Austrian_prisoners_of_war_in_Olonets_province.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Gorskii._Austrian_prisoners_of_war_in_Olonets_province.jpg/440px-Gorskii._Austrian_prisoners_of_war_in_Olonets_province.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3234" data-file-height="2792" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gorskii._Austrian_prisoners_of_war_in_Olonets_province.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_Army" title="Austro-Hungarian Army">Austro-Hungarian</a> POWs in Russia, 1915</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-J28589,_Kriegsgefangene_amerikanische_Soldaten.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-J28589%2C_Kriegsgefangene_amerikanische_Soldaten.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-J28589%2C_Kriegsgefangene_amerikanische_Soldaten.jpg" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-J28589%2C_Kriegsgefangene_amerikanische_Soldaten.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-J28589%2C_Kriegsgefangene_amerikanische_Soldaten.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-J28589%2C_Kriegsgefangene_amerikanische_Soldaten.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-J28589%2C_Kriegsgefangene_amerikanische_Soldaten.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="549" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-J28589,_Kriegsgefangene_amerikanische_Soldaten.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>American prisoners captured by the <a href="/wiki/Wehrmacht" title="Wehrmacht">Wehrmacht</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Ardennes" title="Ardennes">Ardennes</a> in December 1944</div></div></div>\n<p>A <b>prisoner of war</b> (<b>POW</b>) is a person, whether <a href="/wiki/Combatant" title="Combatant">combatant</a> or <a href="/wiki/Non-combatant" title="Non-combatant">non-combatant</a>, who is held in custody by a <a href="/wiki/Belligerent" title="Belligerent">belligerent</a> power during or immediately after an <a href="/wiki/Armed_conflict" class="mw-redirect" title="Armed conflict">armed conflict</a>. The earliest recorded usage of the phrase "prisoner of war" dates to 1660.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Belligerents hold prisoners of war in custody for a range of legitimate and illegitimate reasons, such as isolating them from enemy combatants still in the field (releasing and <a href="/wiki/Repatriation" title="Repatriation">repatriating</a> them in an orderly manner after hostilities), demonstrating military victory, punishing them, prosecuting them for <a href="/wiki/War_crime" title="War crime">war crimes</a>, exploiting them for their labour, recruiting or even <a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">conscripting</a> them as their own combatants, collecting military and political intelligence from them, or <a href="/wiki/Indoctrination" title="Indoctrination">indoctrinating</a> them in new political or religious beliefs.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Treatment_of_POWs_by_the_Axis"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Treatment of POWs by the Axis</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 <span class="toctext">Americans</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#Treatment_of_POWs_by_the_Western_Allies"><span class="tocnumber">5.3</span> <span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-39"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-40"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-41"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_times">Ancient times</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Ancient times">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Abu-Simbel_temple3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Abu-Simbel_temple3.jpg/220px-Abu-Simbel_temple3.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Abu-Simbel_temple3.jpg/330px-Abu-Simbel_temple3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Abu-Simbel_temple3.jpg/440px-Abu-Simbel_temple3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="683" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Abu-Simbel_temple3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Engraving of <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubian</a> prisoners, <a href="/wiki/Abu_Simbel" title="Abu Simbel">Abu Simbel</a>, Egypt, 13th century BC</div></div></div>\n<p>For most of human history, depending on the culture of the victors, enemy combatants on the losing side in a battle who had surrendered and been taken as a prisoner of war could expect to be either slaughtered or <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">enslaved</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The first Roman <a href="/wiki/Gladiator" title="Gladiator">gladiators</a> were prisoners of war and were named according to their ethnic roots such as Samnite, Thracian, and the Gaul (Gallus).<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Homer\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i> describes Greek and Trojan soldiers offering rewards of wealth to opposing forces who have defeated them on the battlefield in exchange for mercy, but their offers are not always accepted; see <a href="/wiki/Lycaon_(Troy)" title="Lycaon (Troy)">Lycaon</a> for example.\n</p><p>Typically, little distinction was made between enemy combatants and enemy civilians, although women and children were more likely to be spared. Sometimes, the purpose of a battle, if not a war, was to capture women, a practice known as <i><a href="/wiki/Raptio" title="Raptio">raptio</a></i>; the <a href="/wiki/Rape_of_the_Sabines" class="mw-redirect" title="Rape of the Sabines">Rape of the Sabines</a> was a large mass abduction by the founders of Rome. Typically women had no <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_rights" title="Women&#39;s rights">rights</a>, and were held legally as <a href="/wiki/Personal_property" title="Personal property">chattel</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the fourth century AD, Bishop <a href="/wiki/Acacius_of_Amida" title="Acacius of Amida">Acacius of Amida</a>, touched by the plight of Persian prisoners captured in a recent war with the Roman Empire, who were held in his town under appalling conditions and destined for a life of slavery, took the initiative of ransoming them, by selling his church\'s precious gold and silver vessels, and letting them return to their country. For this he was eventually canonized.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages_and_Renaissance">Middle Ages and Renaissance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Middle Ages and Renaissance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DiezAlbumsPrisoners.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/DiezAlbumsPrisoners.jpg/220px-DiezAlbumsPrisoners.jpg" width="220" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/DiezAlbumsPrisoners.jpg/330px-DiezAlbumsPrisoners.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/DiezAlbumsPrisoners.jpg/440px-DiezAlbumsPrisoners.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="1204" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DiezAlbumsPrisoners.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol</a> riders with prisoners, 14th century</div></div></div>\n<p>During <a href="/wiki/Childeric_I" title="Childeric I">Childeric</a>\'s siege and blockade of Paris in 464, the nun <a href="/wiki/Genevi%C3%A8ve" class="mw-redirect" title="Genevi\xc3\xa8ve">Genevi\xc3\xa8ve</a> (later canonised as the city\'s patron saint) pleaded with the Frankish king for the welfare of prisoners of war and met with a favourable response. Later, <a href="/wiki/Clovis_I" title="Clovis I">Clovis I</a> liberated captives after Genevieve urged him to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-Attwater_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Attwater-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Many French prisoners of war were killed during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Agincourt" title="Battle of Agincourt">Battle of Agincourt</a> in 1415.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> This was done in retaliation for the French killing of the boys and other non-combatants handling the baggage and equipment of the army, and because the French were attacking again and Henry was afraid that they would break through and free the prisoners to fight again.\n</p><p>In the later <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, a number of <a href="/wiki/Religious_war" title="Religious war">religious wars</a> aimed to not only defeat but eliminate their enemies. In Christian Europe, the extermination of <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heretics</a> was considered desirable. Examples include the 13th century <a href="/wiki/Albigensian_Crusade" title="Albigensian Crusade">Albigensian Crusade</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Crusades" title="Northern Crusades">Northern Crusades</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> When asked by a Crusader how to distinguish between the Catholics and <a href="/wiki/Cathars" class="mw-redirect" title="Cathars">Cathars</a> once they\'d taken the city of <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A9ziers" title="B\xc3\xa9ziers">B\xc3\xa9ziers</a>, the Papal Legate <a href="/wiki/Arnaud_Amalric" title="Arnaud Amalric">Arnaud Amalric</a> famously replied, "<i><a href="/wiki/Caedite_eos._Novit_enim_Dominus_qui_sunt_eius" class="mw-redirect" title="Caedite eos. Novit enim Dominus qui sunt eius">Kill them all, God will know His own</a></i>".<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Likewise, the inhabitants of conquered cities were frequently massacred during the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> in the 11th and 12th centuries. <a href="/wiki/Noblemen" class="mw-redirect" title="Noblemen">Noblemen</a> could hope to be <a href="/wiki/Ransom" title="Ransom">ransomed</a>; their families would have to send to their captors large sums of wealth commensurate with the social status of the captive.\n</p><p>In feudal Japan there was no custom of ransoming prisoners of war, who were for the most part summarily executed.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Codex_Magliabechiano_(141_cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Codex_Magliabechiano_%28141_cropped%29.jpg/220px-Codex_Magliabechiano_%28141_cropped%29.jpg" width="220" height="232" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Codex_Magliabechiano_%28141_cropped%29.jpg/330px-Codex_Magliabechiano_%28141_cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Codex_Magliabechiano_%28141_cropped%29.jpg/440px-Codex_Magliabechiano_%28141_cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1054" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Codex_Magliabechiano_(141_cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aztec sacrifices, <a href="/wiki/Codex_Mendoza" title="Codex Mendoza">Codex Mendoza</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The expanding <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a> was famous for distinguishing between cities or towns that surrendered, where the population were spared but required to support the conquering Mongol army, and those that resisted, where their city was <a href="/wiki/Destruction_under_the_Mongol_Empire" title="Destruction under the Mongol Empire">ransacked and destroyed</a>, and all the population killed. In <a href="/wiki/Termez" title="Termez">Termez</a>, on the <a href="/wiki/Oxus" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxus">Oxus</a>: <i>"all the people, both men and women, were driven out onto the plain, and divided in accordance with their usual custom, then they were all slain"</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Aztec" class="mw-redirect" title="Aztec">Aztecs</a> were constantly at war with neighbouring tribes and groups, with the goal of this constant <a href="/wiki/Aztec_warfare" title="Aztec warfare">warfare</a> being to collect live prisoners for <a href="/wiki/Human_sacrifice" title="Human sacrifice">sacrifice</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> For the re-consecration of <a href="/wiki/Great_Pyramid_of_Tenochtitlan" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Pyramid of Tenochtitlan">Great Pyramid of Tenochtitlan</a> in 1487, "between 10,000 and 80,400 persons" were sacrificed.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Early_Muslim_conquests" title="Early Muslim conquests">early Muslim conquests</a>, Muslims routinely captured large number of prisoners. Aside from those who converted, most were ransomed or enslaved.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Christians who were captured during the Crusades, were usually either killed or sold into slavery if they could not pay a ransom.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> During his lifetime, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> made it the responsibility of the Islamic government to provide food and clothing, on a reasonable basis, to captives, regardless of their religion; however if the prisoners were in the custody of a person, then the responsibility was on the individual.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The freeing of prisoners was highly recommended as a charitable act.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_times">Modern times</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Modern times">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:3090_interrogation_lg.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/3090_interrogation_lg.jpg/220px-3090_interrogation_lg.jpg" width="220" height="126" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/3090_interrogation_lg.jpg/330px-3090_interrogation_lg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/3090_interrogation_lg.jpg/440px-3090_interrogation_lg.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="343" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:3090_interrogation_lg.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Russian and Japanese prisoners being interrogated by Chinese officials during the <a href="/wiki/Boxer_Rebellion" title="Boxer Rebellion">Boxer Rebellion</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The 1648 <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Westphalia" title="Peace of Westphalia">Peace of Westphalia</a>, which ended the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years&#39; War">Thirty Years\' War</a>, established the rule that prisoners of war should be released without ransom at the end of hostilities and that they should be allowed to return to their homelands.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Andersonvillesurvivor.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Andersonvillesurvivor.jpg/170px-Andersonvillesurvivor.jpg" width="170" height="290" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Andersonvillesurvivor.jpg/255px-Andersonvillesurvivor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Andersonvillesurvivor.jpg/340px-Andersonvillesurvivor.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2027" data-file-height="3456" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Andersonvillesurvivor.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Union_Army" title="Union Army">Union Army</a> soldier on his release from <a href="/wiki/Andersonville_prison" class="mw-redirect" title="Andersonville prison">Andersonville prison</a> in May 1865.</div></div></div>\n<p>There also evolved the <a href="/wiki/Prisoners_of_war_parole" class="mw-redirect" title="Prisoners of war parole">right of <i>parole</i></a>, French for "discourse", in which a captured officer surrendered his sword and gave his word as a gentleman in exchange for privileges. If he swore not to escape, he could gain better accommodations and the freedom of the prison. If he swore to cease hostilities against the nation who held him captive, he could be repatriated or exchanged but could not serve against his former captors in a military capacity.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_settlers_captured_in_North_America">European settlers captured in North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: European settlers captured in North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution_prisoners_of_war" class="mw-redirect" title="American Revolution prisoners of war">American Revolution prisoners of war</a></div>\n<p>Early historical narratives of captured colonial Europeans, including perspectives of literate women captured by the indigenous peoples of North America, exist in some number. The writings of <a href="/wiki/Mary_Rowlandson" title="Mary Rowlandson">Mary Rowlandson</a>, captured in the brutal fighting of <a href="/wiki/King_Philip%27s_War" title="King Philip&#39;s War">King Philip\'s War</a>, are an example. Such narratives enjoyed some popularity, spawning a genre of the <a href="/wiki/Captivity_narrative" title="Captivity narrative">captivity narrative</a>, and had lasting influence on the body of early American literature, most notably through the legacy of <a href="/wiki/James_Fenimore_Cooper" title="James Fenimore Cooper">James Fenimore Cooper</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Last_of_the_Mohicans" class="mw-redirect" title="Last of the Mohicans">Last of the Mohicans</a></i>. Some Native Americans continued to capture Europeans and use them both as labourers and bargaining chips into the 19th century; see for example <a href="/wiki/John_R._Jewitt" title="John R. Jewitt">John R. Jewitt</a>, an Englishman who wrote a memoir about his years as a captive of the <a href="/wiki/Nuu-chah-nulth_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuu-chah-nulth people">Nootka</a> people on the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Northwest" title="Pacific Northwest">Pacific Northwest</a> coast from 1802\xe2\x80\x931805.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="French_Revolutionary_wars_and_Napoleonic_wars">French Revolutionary wars and Napoleonic wars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: French Revolutionary wars and Napoleonic wars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The earliest known purposely built <a href="/wiki/Prisoner-of-war_camp" title="Prisoner-of-war camp">prisoner-of-war camp</a> was established at <a href="/wiki/Norman_Cross" title="Norman Cross">Norman Cross</a>, England in 1797 to house the increasing number of prisoners from the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolutionary_Wars" title="French Revolutionary Wars">French Revolutionary Wars</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The average prison population was about 5,500 men. The lowest number recorded was 3,300 in October 1804 and 6,272 on 10 April 1810 was the highest number of prisoners recorded in any official document. Norman Cross was intended to be a model depot providing the most humane treatment of prisoners of war. The British government went to great lengths to provide food of a quality at least equal to that available to locals. The senior officer from each quadrangle was permitted to inspect the food as it was delivered to the prison to ensure it was of sufficient quality. Despite the generous supply and quality of food, some prisoners died of starvation after gambling away their rations. Most of the men held in the prison were low-ranking soldiers and sailors, including midshipmen and junior officers, with a small number of <a href="/wiki/Privateers" class="mw-redirect" title="Privateers">privateers</a>. About 100 senior officers and some civilians "of good social standing", mainly passengers on captured ships and the wives of some officers, were given <i>parole d\'honneur</i> outside the prison, mainly in <a href="/wiki/Peterborough" title="Peterborough">Peterborough</a> although some further afield in <a href="/wiki/Northampton" title="Northampton">Northampton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plymouth" title="Plymouth">Plymouth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Melrose,_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Melrose, Scotland">Melrose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abergavenny" title="Abergavenny">Abergavenny</a>. They were afforded the courtesy of their rank within English society.\nThe Leipzig citizen <a href="/wiki/Johann_Friedrich_Rochlitz" title="Johann Friedrich Rochlitz">Rochlitz</a> remarked in his account about the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Leipzig" title="Battle of Leipzig">Battle of Leipzig</a>, that large crowds of French POWs were held on fields outside the town, begged passersby for food, and that most of them didn\'t survive this ordeal.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prisoner_exchanges">Prisoner exchanges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Prisoner exchanges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The extensive period of conflict during the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a> and <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a> (1793\xe2\x80\x931815), followed by the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_%E2%80%93_United_States_relations" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom \xe2\x80\x93 United States relations">Anglo-American</a> <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>, led to the emergence of a <a href="/wiki/Cartel_(ship)" title="Cartel (ship)">cartel</a> system for the <a href="/wiki/Exchange_of_prisoners" class="mw-redirect" title="Exchange of prisoners">exchange of prisoners</a>, even while the belligerents were at war. A cartel was usually arranged by the respective armed service for the exchange of like-ranked personnel. The aim was to achieve a reduction in the number of prisoners held, while at the same time alleviating shortages of skilled personnel in the home country.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="American_Civil_War">American Civil War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: American Civil War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War_prison_camps" title="American Civil War prison camps">American Civil War prison camps</a></div>\n<p>At the start of the civil war a system of paroles operated. Captives agreed not to fight until they were officially exchanged. Meanwhile, they were held in camps run by their own army where they were paid but not allowed to perform any military duties.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The system of exchanges collapsed in 1863 when the Confederacy refused to exchange black prisoners. In the late summer of 1864, a year after the <a href="/wiki/Dix-Hill_Cartel" class="mw-redirect" title="Dix-Hill Cartel">Dix-Hill Cartel</a> was suspended; Confederate officials approached Union General Benjamin Butler, Union Commissioner of Exchange, about resuming the cartel and including the black prisoners. Butler contacted Grant for guidance on the issue, and Grant responded to Butler on August 18, 1864 with his now famous statement. He rejected the offer, stating in essence, that the Union could afford to leave their men in captivity, the Confederacy could not.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> After that about 56,000 of the 409,000 POWs died in prisons during the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, accounting for nearly 10% of the conflict\'s fatalities.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Of the 45,000 Union prisoners of war confined in <a href="/wiki/Camp_Sumter" class="mw-redirect" title="Camp Sumter">Camp Sumter</a>, located near <a href="/wiki/Andersonville,_Georgia" title="Andersonville, Georgia">Andersonville, Georgia</a>, 13,000 (28%) died.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Camp_Douglas_(Chicago)" title="Camp Douglas (Chicago)">Camp Douglas</a> in Chicago, Illinois, 10% of its Confederate prisoners died during one cold winter month; and <a href="/wiki/Elmira_Prison" title="Elmira Prison">Elmira Prison</a> in New York state, with a death rate of 25% (2,963), nearly equalled that of Andersonville.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Amelioration">Amelioration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Amelioration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>During the 19th century, there were increased efforts to improve the treatment and processing of prisoners. As a result of these emerging conventions, a number of international conferences were held, starting with the Brussels Conference of 1874, with nations agreeing that it was necessary to prevent inhumane treatment of prisoners and the use of weapons causing unnecessary harm. Although no agreements were immediately ratified by the participating nations, work was continued that resulted in new <a href="/wiki/Treaty" title="Treaty">conventions</a> being adopted and becoming recognized as <a href="/wiki/International_law" title="International law">international law</a> that specified that prisoners of war be treated humanely and diplomatically.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hague_and_Geneva_Conventions">Hague and Geneva Conventions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Hague and Geneva Conventions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Chapter II of the Annex to the <a href="/wiki/Hague_Convention_of_1907" class="mw-redirect" title="Hague Convention of 1907">1907 Hague Convention</a> <i>IV \xe2\x80\x93 The Laws and Customs of War on Land</i> covered the treatment of prisoners of war in detail. These provisions were further expanded in the <a href="/wiki/Geneva_Convention_(1929)" title="Geneva Convention (1929)">1929 Geneva Convention on the Prisoners of War</a> and were largely revised in the <a href="/wiki/Third_Geneva_Convention" title="Third Geneva Convention">Third Geneva Convention</a> in 1949.\n</p><p>Article 4 of the <a href="/wiki/Third_Geneva_Convention" title="Third Geneva Convention">Third Geneva Convention</a> protects captured <a href="/wiki/Military_personnel" title="Military personnel">military personnel</a>, some <a href="/wiki/Guerrilla_warfare" title="Guerrilla warfare">guerrilla</a> fighters, and certain <a href="/wiki/Civilians" class="mw-redirect" title="Civilians">civilians</a>. It applies from the moment a prisoner is captured until he or she is released or repatriated. One of the main provisions of the convention makes it illegal to <a href="/wiki/Torture" title="Torture">torture</a> prisoners and states that a prisoner can only be required to give their <a href="/wiki/Name" title="Name">name</a>, <a href="/wiki/Date_of_birth" class="mw-redirect" title="Date of birth">date of birth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Military_rank" title="Military rank">rank</a> and <a href="/wiki/Service_number" title="Service number">service number</a> (if applicable).\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/ICRC" class="mw-redirect" title="ICRC">ICRC</a> has a special role to play, with regards to <a href="/wiki/International_humanitarian_law" title="International humanitarian law">international humanitarian law</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Restoring_Family_Links" title="Restoring Family Links">restoring and maintaining family contact in times of war</a>, in particular concerning the right of prisoners of war and internees to send and receive letters and cards (Geneva Convention (GC) III, art.71 and GC IV, art.107).\n</p><p>However, nations vary in their dedication to following these laws, and historically the treatment of POWs has varied greatly. During World War II, <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Japan" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperial Japan">Imperial Japan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> (towards Soviet POWs and Western Allied commandos) were notorious for atrocities against prisoners of war. The German military used the Soviet Union\'s refusal to sign the Geneva Convention as a reason for not providing the necessities of life to Soviet POWs; and the Soviets similarly killed Axis prisoners or used them as slave labour. The Germans also routinely executed Western Allied commandos captured behind German lines per the <a href="/wiki/Commando_Order" title="Commando Order">Commando Order</a>. North Korean and North and South Vietnamese forces<sup id="cite_ref-south-viet_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-south-viet-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> routinely killed or mistreated prisoners taken during those conflicts.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Qualifications">Qualifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Qualifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Japanese_Beheading_1894.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Japanese_Beheading_1894.jpg/220px-Japanese_Beheading_1894.jpg" width="220" height="109" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Japanese_Beheading_1894.jpg/330px-Japanese_Beheading_1894.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Japanese_Beheading_1894.jpg/440px-Japanese_Beheading_1894.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1306" data-file-height="650" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Japanese_Beheading_1894.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Japanese illustration depicting the beheading of Chinese captives. <a href="/wiki/First_Sino-Japanese_War" title="First Sino-Japanese War">Sino-Japanese War</a> of 1894\xe2\x80\x935.</div></div></div>\n<p>To be entitled to prisoner-of-war status, captured persons must be lawful combatants entitled to combatant\'s privilege\xe2\x80\x94which gives them immunity from punishment for crimes constituting lawful acts of war such as killing enemy combatants. To qualify under the Third Geneva Convention, a combatant must be part of a <a href="/wiki/Chain_of_command" class="mw-redirect" title="Chain of command">chain of command</a>, wear a "fixed distinctive marking, visible from a distance", bear arms openly, and have conducted military operations according to the <a href="/wiki/Laws_and_customs_of_war" class="mw-redirect" title="Laws and customs of war">laws and customs of war</a>. (The Convention recognizes a few other groups as well, such as "[i]nhabitants of a non-occupied territory, who on the approach of the enemy spontaneously take up arms to resist the invading forces, without having had time to form themselves into regular armed units".)\n</p><p>Thus, uniforms and/or badges are important in determining prisoner-of-war status; and <i><a href="/wiki/Francs-tireurs" title="Francs-tireurs">francs-tireurs</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Terrorists" class="mw-redirect" title="Terrorists">terrorists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sabotage" title="Sabotage">saboteurs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mercenary" title="Mercenary">mercenaries</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Espionage" title="Espionage">spies</a> do not qualify because they do not always follow the laws and customs of war and therefore they fall under the category of <a href="/wiki/Unlawful_combatant" title="Unlawful combatant">unlawful combatants</a>. In practice, these criteria are rarely interpreted strictly. <a href="/wiki/Guerrilla" class="mw-redirect" title="Guerrilla">Guerrillas</a>, for example, usually do not wear a uniform or carry arms openly, but captured guerrillas are often granted POW status.\n</p><p>The criteria are applied primarily to <i>international</i> armed conflicts; in <a href="/wiki/Civil_wars" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil wars">civil wars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Insurgents" class="mw-redirect" title="Insurgents">insurgents</a> are often treated as <a href="/wiki/Traitors" class="mw-redirect" title="Traitors">traitors</a> or <a href="/wiki/Criminals" class="mw-redirect" title="Criminals">criminals</a> by government forces, and are sometimes executed. However, in the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, both sides treated captured troops as POWs, presumably out of <a href="/wiki/Reciprocity_(international_relations)" title="Reciprocity (international relations)">reciprocity</a>, although the <a href="/wiki/Union_(American_Civil_War)" title="Union (American Civil War)">Union</a> regarded <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate</a> personnel as separatist rebels. However, guerrillas and other irregular combatants generally cannot expect to receive benefits from both civilian and military status simultaneously.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rights">Rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Under the <a href="/wiki/Third_Geneva_Convention" title="Third Geneva Convention">Third Geneva Convention</a>, prisoners of war (POW) must be:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Treated humanely with respect for their persons and their honor</li>\n<li>Able to inform their next of kin and the <a href="/wiki/International_Committee_of_the_Red_Cross" title="International Committee of the Red Cross">International Committee of the Red Cross</a> of their capture</li>\n<li>Allowed to communicate regularly with relatives and receive packages</li>\n<li>Given adequate food, clothing, housing, and medical attention</li>\n<li>Paid for work done and not forced to do work that is dangerous, unhealthy, or degrading</li>\n<li>Released quickly after conflicts end</li>\n<li>Not compelled to give any information except for name, age, rank, and service number<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>In addition, if wounded or sick on the battlefield, the prisoner will receive help from the <a href="/wiki/International_Committee_of_the_Red_Cross" title="International Committee of the Red Cross">International Committee of the Red Cross</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>When a country is responsible for breaches of prisoner of war rights, those accountable will be punished accordingly. An example of this is the <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Trials" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuremberg Trials">Nuremberg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Trials" class="mw-redirect" title="Tokyo Trials">Tokyo Trials</a>. German and Japanese military commanders were prosecuted for preparing and initiating a <a href="/wiki/War_of_aggression" title="War of aggression">war of aggression</a>, <a href="/wiki/Murder" title="Murder">murder</a>, ill treatment, and <a href="/wiki/Deportation" title="Deportation">deportation</a> of individuals, and <a href="/wiki/Genocide" title="Genocide">genocide</a> during World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Most were executed or sentenced to life in prison for their crimes.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._Code_of_Conduct_and_terminology">U.S. Code of Conduct and terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: U.S. Code of Conduct and terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:VietnamCombatArtCATJamesPollockWaitingInterrogation.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/VietnamCombatArtCATJamesPollockWaitingInterrogation.jpg/220px-VietnamCombatArtCATJamesPollockWaitingInterrogation.jpg" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/VietnamCombatArtCATJamesPollockWaitingInterrogation.jpg/330px-VietnamCombatArtCATJamesPollockWaitingInterrogation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3a/VietnamCombatArtCATJamesPollockWaitingInterrogation.jpg 2x" data-file-width="336" data-file-height="504" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:VietnamCombatArtCATJamesPollockWaitingInterrogation.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Waiting interrogation, 199th LT INF BG</i> by <a href="/wiki/James_Pollock_(artist)" title="James Pollock (artist)">James Pollock</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/The_United_States_Military_Code_of_Conduct" class="mw-redirect" title="The United States Military Code of Conduct">The United States Military Code of Conduct</a> was promulgated in 1955 via <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Executive_Order_10631" class="extiw" title="s:Executive Order 10631">Executive Order 10631</a> under <a href="/wiki/U.S._President" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. President">President</a> <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a> to serve as a moral code for United States service members who have been taken prisoner. It was created primarily in response to the breakdown of leadership and organization, specifically when U.S. forces were POWs during the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>.\n</p><p>When a military member is taken prisoner, the Code of Conduct reminds them that the chain of command is still in effect (the highest ranking service member eligible for command, regardless of service branch, is in command), and requires them to support their leadership. The Code of Conduct also requires service members to resist giving information to the enemy (beyond identifying themselves, that is, "name, rank, serial number"), receiving special favors or parole, or otherwise providing their enemy captors aid and comfort.\n</p><p>Since the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>, the official U.S. military term for enemy POWs is EPW (Enemy Prisoner of War). This name change was introduced in order to distinguish between enemy and U.S. captives.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2000, the U.S. military replaced the designation "Prisoner of War" for captured American personnel with "Missing-Captured". A January 2008 directive states that the reasoning behind this is since "Prisoner of War" is the international legal recognized status for such people there is no need for any individual country to follow suit. This change remains relatively unknown even among experts in the field and "Prisoner of War" remains widely used in the Pentagon which has a "POW/Missing Personnel Office" and awards the <a href="/wiki/Prisoner_of_War_Medal" title="Prisoner of War Medal">Prisoner of War Medal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Missing-Captured_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Missing-Captured-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_I">World War I</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: World War I">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/World_War_I_prisoners_of_war_in_Germany" title="World War I prisoners of war in Germany">World War I prisoners of war in Germany</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_pow.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/US_pow.jpg/220px-US_pow.jpg" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/US_pow.jpg/330px-US_pow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/US_pow.jpg/440px-US_pow.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="336" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_pow.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>American prisoners of war in Germany in 1917</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:German_POWs_captured_in_Flanders_by_Brits2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/German_POWs_captured_in_Flanders_by_Brits2.jpg/220px-German_POWs_captured_in_Flanders_by_Brits2.jpg" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/German_POWs_captured_in_Flanders_by_Brits2.jpg/330px-German_POWs_captured_in_Flanders_by_Brits2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/German_POWs_captured_in_Flanders_by_Brits2.jpg/440px-German_POWs_captured_in_Flanders_by_Brits2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="822" data-file-height="639" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:German_POWs_captured_in_Flanders_by_Brits2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>German soldiers captured by the British in <a href="/wiki/Flanders" title="Flanders">Flanders</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>During World War I, about eight million men surrendered and were held in POW camps until the war ended. All nations pledged to follow the Hague rules on fair treatment of prisoners of war, and in general the POWs had a much higher survival rate than their peers who were not captured.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Individual surrenders were uncommon; usually a large unit surrendered all its men. At <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tannenberg_(1914)" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Tannenberg (1914)">Tannenberg</a> 92,000 Russians surrendered during the battle. When the besieged garrison of <a href="/wiki/Kaunas" title="Kaunas">Kaunas</a> surrendered in 1915, 20,000 Russians became prisoners. Over half the Russian losses were prisoners as a proportion of those captured, wounded or killed. About 3.3 million men became prisoners.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a> held 2.5 million prisoners; <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russia</a> held 2.9 million, and <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">Britain</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_Third_Republic" title="French Third Republic">France</a> held about 720,000, mostly gained in the period just before the <a href="/wiki/Armistice" title="Armistice">Armistice</a> in 1918. The US held 48,000. The most dangerous moment for POW\'s was the act of surrender, when helpless soldiers were sometimes mistakenly shot down. Once prisoners reached a POW camp conditions were better (and often much better than in World War II), thanks in part to the efforts of the <a href="/wiki/International_Red_Cross" class="mw-redirect" title="International Red Cross">International Red Cross</a> and inspections by neutral nations.\n</p><p>There was however much harsh treatment of POWs in Germany, as recorded by the American ambassador to Germany (prior to America\'s entry into the war), James W. Gerard, who published his findings in "My Four Years in Germany". Even worse conditions are reported in the book "Escape of a Princess Pat" by the Canadian George Pearson. It was particularly bad in Russia, where starvation was common for prisoners and civilians alike; a quarter of the over 2 million POWs held there died.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Nearly 375,000 of the 500,000 <a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Austro-Hungarian">Austro-Hungarian</a> prisoners of war taken by Russians perished in <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> from <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Typhus" title="Typhus">typhus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> In Germany, food was short, but only 5% died.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> often treated prisoners of war poorly. Some 11,800 British soldiers, most of them Indians, became prisoners after the five-month <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Kut" title="Siege of Kut">Siege of Kut</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, in April 1916. Many were weak and starved when they surrendered and 4,250 died in captivity.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Sinai_and_Palestine_campaign" class="mw-redirect" title="Sinai and Palestine campaign">Sinai and Palestine campaign</a> 217 Australian and unknown numbers of British, New Zealand and Indian soldiers were captured by Ottoman Empire forces. About 50% of the Australian prisoners were light horsemen including 48 missing believed captured on 1 May 1918 in the Jordan Valley. <a href="/wiki/Australian_Flying_Corps" title="Australian Flying Corps">Australian Flying Corps</a> pilots and observers were captured in the Sinai Peninsula, Palestine and the Levant. One third of all Australian prisoners were captured on Gallipoli including the crew of the submarine AE2 which made a passage through the Dardanelles in 1915. Forced marches and crowded railway journeys preceded years in camps where disease, poor diet and inadequate medical facilities prevailed. About 25% of other ranks died, many from malnutrition, while only one officer died.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The most curious case came in Russia where the <a href="/wiki/Czechoslovak_Legion" title="Czechoslovak Legion">Czechoslovak Legion</a> of <a href="/wiki/Czechoslovak" class="mw-redirect" title="Czechoslovak">Czechoslovak</a> prisoners (from the <a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Austro-Hungarian">Austro-Hungarian</a> army): they were released in 1917, armed themselves, briefly culminating into a military and diplomatic force during the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Civil_War" title="Russian Civil War">Russian Civil War</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Release_of_prisoners">Release of prisoners</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Release of prisoners">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kriegsgefangenen-Ehrenmal-Bremen.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Kriegsgefangenen-Ehrenmal-Bremen.JPG/220px-Kriegsgefangenen-Ehrenmal-Bremen.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Kriegsgefangenen-Ehrenmal-Bremen.JPG/330px-Kriegsgefangenen-Ehrenmal-Bremen.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Kriegsgefangenen-Ehrenmal-Bremen.JPG/440px-Kriegsgefangenen-Ehrenmal-Bremen.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kriegsgefangenen-Ehrenmal-Bremen.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A memorial to German prisoners of war who died in 1914\xe2\x80\x931920</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1971-085-63,_Begr%C3%BC%C3%9Fungsfeier_f%C3%BCr_heimgekehrte_Kriegsgefangene,_Berlin.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1971-085-63%2C_Begr%C3%BC%C3%9Fungsfeier_f%C3%BCr_heimgekehrte_Kriegsgefangene%2C_Berlin.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1971-085-63%2C_Begr%C3%BC%C3%9Fungsfeier_f%C3%BCr_heimgekehrte_Kriegsgefangene%2C_Berlin.jpg" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1971-085-63%2C_Begr%C3%BC%C3%9Fungsfeier_f%C3%BCr_heimgekehrte_Kriegsgefangene%2C_Berlin.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1971-085-63%2C_Begr%C3%BC%C3%9Fungsfeier_f%C3%BCr_heimgekehrte_Kriegsgefangene%2C_Berlin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1971-085-63%2C_Begr%C3%BC%C3%9Fungsfeier_f%C3%BCr_heimgekehrte_Kriegsgefangene%2C_Berlin.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1971-085-63%2C_Begr%C3%BC%C3%9Fungsfeier_f%C3%BCr_heimgekehrte_Kriegsgefangene%2C_Berlin.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="554" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1971-085-63,_Begr%C3%BC%C3%9Fungsfeier_f%C3%BCr_heimgekehrte_Kriegsgefangene,_Berlin.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Celebration for returning POWs, Berlin 1920</div></div></div>\n<p>At the end of the war in 1918 there were believed to be 140,000 British prisoners of war in Germany, including thousands of internees held in neutral Switzerland.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The first British prisoners were released and reached <a href="/wiki/Calais" title="Calais">Calais</a> on 15 November. Plans were made for them to be sent via <a href="/wiki/Dunkirk" title="Dunkirk">Dunkirk</a> to <a href="/wiki/Dover" title="Dover">Dover</a> and a large reception camp was established at Dover capable of housing 40,000 men, which could later be used for <a href="/wiki/Demobilisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Demobilisation">demobilisation</a>.\n</p><p>On 13 December 1918, the armistice was extended and the Allies reported that by 9 December 264,000 prisoners had been repatriated. A very large number of these had been released <i>en masse</i> and sent across Allied lines without any food or shelter. This created difficulties for the receiving Allies and many released prisoners died from exhaustion. The released POWs were met by <a href="/wiki/Cavalry" title="Cavalry">cavalry</a> troops and sent back through the lines in lorries to reception centres where they were refitted with boots and clothing and dispatched to the ports in trains.\n</p><p>Upon arrival at the receiving camp the POWs were registered and "boarded" before being dispatched to their own homes. All <a href="/wiki/Commissioned_officer" class="mw-redirect" title="Commissioned officer">commissioned officers</a> had to write a report on the circumstances of their capture and to ensure that they had done all they could to avoid capture. Each returning officer and man was given a message from King <a href="/wiki/George_V_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="George V of the United Kingdom">George V</a>, written in his own hand and reproduced on a lithograph. It read as follows:<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The Queen joins me in welcoming you on your release from the miseries &amp; hardships, which you have endured with so much patience and courage.\n</p><p>During these many months of trial, the early rescue of our gallant Officers &amp; Men from the cruelties of their captivity has been uppermost in our thoughts.\n</p><p>We are thankful that this longed for day has arrived, &amp; that back in the old Country you will be able once more to enjoy the happiness of a home &amp; to see good days among those who anxiously look for your return.\nGeorge R.I.\n</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>While the Allied prisoners were sent home at the end of the war, the same treatment was not granted to <a href="/wiki/Central_Powers" title="Central Powers">Central Powers</a> prisoners of the Allies and Russia, many of whom had to serve as <a href="/wiki/Forced_labour" class="mw-redirect" title="Forced labour">forced labour</a>, e.g. in France, until 1920. They were released after many approaches by the <a href="/wiki/ICRC" class="mw-redirect" title="ICRC">ICRC</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Allied_Supreme_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Allied Supreme Council">Allied Supreme Council</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II">World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-267-0111-36,_Russland,_russische_Kriegsgefangene_(Juden).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-267-0111-36%2C_Russland%2C_russische_Kriegsgefangene_%28Juden%29.jpg/170px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-267-0111-36%2C_Russland%2C_russische_Kriegsgefangene_%28Juden%29.jpg" width="170" height="265" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-267-0111-36%2C_Russland%2C_russische_Kriegsgefangene_%28Juden%29.jpg/255px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-267-0111-36%2C_Russland%2C_russische_Kriegsgefangene_%28Juden%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-267-0111-36%2C_Russland%2C_russische_Kriegsgefangene_%28Juden%29.jpg/340px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-267-0111-36%2C_Russland%2C_russische_Kriegsgefangene_%28Juden%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="513" data-file-height="799" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-267-0111-36,_Russland,_russische_Kriegsgefangene_(Juden).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jewish Soviet POW captured by the German Army, August 1941. At least 50,000 Jewish soldiers were shot after selection.</div></div></div>\n<p>Historian <a href="/wiki/Niall_Ferguson" title="Niall Ferguson">Niall Ferguson</a>, in addition to figures from <a href="/wiki/Keith_Lowe_(author)" title="Keith Lowe (author)">Keith Lowe</a>, tabulated the total death rate for POWs in World War II as follows:<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><table class="wikitable sortable">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>&#160;</th>\n<th>Percentage of<br />POWs that Died\n</th></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td style="text-align:left;">Soviet POWs held by Germans</td>\n<td>57.5%\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td style="text-align:left;">German POWs held by Yugoslavs</td>\n<td>41.2%\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td style="text-align:left;">German POWs held by Soviets</td>\n<td>35.8%\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td style="text-align:left;">American POWs held by Japanese</td>\n<td>33.0%\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td style="text-align:left;">American POWs held by Germans</td>\n<td>1.19%\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td style="text-align:left;">German POWs held by Eastern Europeans</td>\n<td>32.9%\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td style="text-align:left;">British POWs held by Japanese</td>\n<td>24.8%\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td style="text-align:left;">German POWs held by Czechoslovaks</td>\n<td>5.0%\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td style="text-align:left;">British POWs held by Germans</td>\n<td>3.5%\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td style="text-align:left;">German POWs held by French</td>\n<td>2.58%\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td style="text-align:left;">German POWs held by Americans</td>\n<td>0.15%\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td style="text-align:left;">German POWs held by British</td>\n<td>0.03%\n</td></tr></tbody></table></dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment_of_POWs_by_the_Axis">Treatment of POWs by the Axis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Treatment of POWs by the Axis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Nazi_crimes_against_Soviet_POWs" class="mw-redirect" title="Nazi crimes against Soviet POWs">Nazi crimes against Soviet POWs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_war_crimes" title="Japanese war crimes">Japanese war crimes</a></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Empire_of_Japan">Empire of Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Empire of Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Empire of Japan</a>, which had signed but never ratified the <a href="/wiki/Geneva_Convention_(1929)" title="Geneva Convention (1929)">1929 Geneva Convention on Prisoners of War</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> did not treat prisoners of war in accordance with international agreements, including provisions of the <a href="/wiki/Hague_Conventions_(1899_and_1907)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hague Conventions (1899 and 1907)">Hague Conventions</a>, either during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Sino-Japanese_War" title="Second Sino-Japanese War">Second Sino-Japanese War</a> or during the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_War" title="Pacific War">Pacific War</a>, because the Japanese viewed surrender as dishonorable. Moreover, according to a directive ratified on 5 August 1937 by <a href="/wiki/Hirohito" title="Hirohito">Hirohito</a>, the constraints of the Hague Conventions were explicitly removed on Chinese prisoners.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Prisoners of war from China, the United States, Australia, Britain, Canada, India, the Netherlands, New Zealand, and the Philippines held by the Japanese armed forces were subject to murder, beatings, summary punishment, brutal treatment, <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_Japan" title="Slavery in Japan">forced labour</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unit_731" title="Unit 731">medical experimentation</a>, starvation rations, poor medical treatment and <a href="/wiki/Chichijima_incident" title="Chichijima incident">cannibalism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> The most notorious use of forced labour was in the construction of the Burma\xe2\x80\x93Thailand <a href="/wiki/Death_Railway" class="mw-redirect" title="Death Railway">Death Railway</a>. After 20 March 1943, the Imperial Navy was under orders to execute all prisoners taken at sea.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to the findings of the <a href="/wiki/International_Military_Tribunal_for_the_Far_East" title="International Military Tribunal for the Far East">Tokyo Tribunal</a>, the death rate of Western prisoners was 27.1%, seven times that of POWs under the Germans and Italians.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The death rate of Chinese was much higher. Thus, while 37,583 prisoners from the United Kingdom, Commonwealth, and Dominions, 28,500 from the Netherlands, and 14,473 from the United States were released after the <a href="/wiki/Surrender_of_Japan" title="Surrender of Japan">surrender of Japan</a>, the number for the Chinese was only 56.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The 27,465 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">United States Army</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Air_Forces" title="United States Army Air Forces">United States Army Air Forces</a> POWs in the Pacific Theater had a 40.4% death rate.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The War Ministry in Tokyo issued an order at the end of the war to kill all surviving POWs.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>No direct access to the POWs was provided to the <a href="/wiki/International_Red_Cross" class="mw-redirect" title="International Red Cross">International Red Cross</a>. Escapes among Caucasian prisoners were almost impossible because of the difficulty of men of Caucasian descent hiding in Asiatic societies.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Allied POW camps and ship-transports were sometimes accidental targets of Allied attacks. The number of deaths which occurred when Japanese "<a href="/wiki/Hell_ship" title="Hell ship">hell ships</a>"\xe2\x80\x94unmarked transport ships in which POWs were transported in harsh conditions\xe2\x80\x94were attacked by US Navy submarines was particularly high. Gavan Daws has calculated that "of all POWs who died in the Pacific War, one in three was killed on the water by friendly fire".<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Daves states that 10,800 of the 50,000 POWs shipped by the Japanese were killed at sea<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> while Donald L. Miller states that "approximately 21,000 Allied POWs died at sea, about 19,000 of them killed by friendly fire."<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Life in the POW camps was recorded at great risk to themselves by artists such as <a href="/wiki/Jack_Bridger_Chalker" title="Jack Bridger Chalker">Jack Bridger Chalker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philip_Meninsky" title="Philip Meninsky">Philip Meninsky</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ashley_George_Old" title="Ashley George Old">Ashley George Old</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Searle" title="Ronald Searle">Ronald Searle</a>. Human hair was often used for brushes, plant juices and blood for paint, and toilet paper as the "canvas". Some of their works were used as evidence in the trials of Japanese war criminals.\n</p><p>Research into the conditions of the camps has been conducted by The Liverpool School of Tropical Medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div
