Japanese.\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany">Germany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Germany">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="French_soldiers">French soldiers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: French soldiers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/French_prisoners_of_war_in_World_War_II" title="French prisoners of war in World War II">French prisoners of war in World War II</a></div>\n<p>After the French armies surrendered in summer 1940, Germany seized two million French prisoners of war and sent them to camps in Germany. About one third were released on various terms. Of the remainder, the officers and non-commissioned officers were kept in camps and did not work. The privates were sent out to work. About half of them worked for German agriculture, where food supplies were adequate and controls were lenient. The others worked in factories or mines, where conditions were much harsher.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span id="Western_Allies.27_POWs"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_Allies\'_POWs">Western Allies\' POWs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Western Allies&#039; POWs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Belgian_prisoners_of_war_in_World_War_II" title="Belgian prisoners of war in World War II">Belgian prisoners of war in World War II</a></div>\n<p>Germany and Italy generally treated prisoners from the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">British Commonwealth</a>, France, the USA, and other western Allies in accordance with the <a href="/wiki/Geneva_Convention_(1929)" title="Geneva Convention (1929)">Geneva Convention</a>, which had been signed by these countries.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Consequently, western Allied officers were not usually made to work and some personnel of lower rank were usually compensated, or not required to work either. The main complaints of western Allied prisoners of war in <a href="/wiki/Wehrmacht" title="Wehrmacht">German</a> POW camps\xe2\x80\x94especially during the last two years of the war\xe2\x80\x94concerned shortages of food.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:American_POWs_AF_Museum.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/American_POWs_AF_Museum.jpg/220px-American_POWs_AF_Museum.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/American_POWs_AF_Museum.jpg/330px-American_POWs_AF_Museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/American_POWs_AF_Museum.jpg/440px-American_POWs_AF_Museum.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1800" data-file-height="1197" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:American_POWs_AF_Museum.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Representation of a "Forty-and-eight" boxcar used to transport American POWs in Germany during World War II.</div></div></div>\n<p>Only a small proportion of western Allied POWs who were <a href="/wiki/Jew" class="mw-redirect" title="Jew">Jews</a>\xe2\x80\x94or whom the Nazis believed to be Jewish\xe2\x80\x94were killed as part of <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">the Holocaust</a> or were subjected to other <a href="/wiki/Antisemitism" title="Antisemitism">antisemitic</a> policies.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (July 2016)">dubious</span></a>&#32;<span class="metadata"> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/Talk:Prisoner_of_war#Dubious" title="Talk:Prisoner of war">discuss</a></span></i>&#93;</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> For example, Major <a href="/wiki/Yitzhak_Ben-Aharon" title="Yitzhak Ben-Aharon">Yitzhak Ben-Aharon</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_Jew" class="mw-redirect" title="Palestinian Jew">Palestinian Jew</a> who had enlisted in the British Army, and who was captured by the Germans in <a href="/wiki/Greek_Campaign" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Campaign">Greece in 1941</a>, experienced four years of captivity under entirely normal conditions for POWs.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pow_telegram.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Pow_telegram.jpg/220px-Pow_telegram.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Pow_telegram.jpg/330px-Pow_telegram.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Pow_telegram.jpg/440px-Pow_telegram.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pow_telegram.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Telegram notifying parents of an American POW of his capture by Germany</div></div></div>\n<p>However, a small number of Allied personnel were sent to concentration camps, for a variety of reasons including being Jewish.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> As the US historian Joseph Robert White put it: "An important exception ... is the sub-camp for U.S. POWs at <a href="/wiki/Berga,_Thuringia" title="Berga, Thuringia">Berga an der Elster</a>, officially called <i><a href="/wiki/Arbeitslager" title="Arbeitslager">Arbeitskommando</a> 625</i> [also known as <i><a href="/wiki/Stalag_IX-B" title="Stalag IX-B">Stalag IX-B</a></i>]. Berga was the deadliest work detachment for American captives in Germany. 73 men who participated, or 21 percent of the detachment, perished in two months. 80 of the 350 POWs were Jews."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>  Another well-known example was a group of 168 Australian, British, Canadian, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> and US aviators who were held for two months at <a href="/wiki/Buchenwald_concentration_camp" title="Buchenwald concentration camp">Buchenwald concentration camp</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> two of the POWs died at Buchenwald. Two possible reasons have been suggested for this incident: German authorities wanted to make an example of <i><a href="/wiki/Terror_bombing" class="mw-redirect" title="Terror bombing">Terrorflieger</a></i> ("terrorist aviators") and/or these aircrews were classified as spies, because they had been disguised as civilians or enemy soldiers when they were apprehended.\n</p><p>Information on conditions in the stalags is contradictory depending on the source. Some American POWs claimed the Germans were victims of circumstance and did the best they could, while others accused their captors of brutalities and forced labour. In any case, the prison camps were miserable places where food rations were meager and conditions squalid. One American admitted "The only difference between the stalags and concentration camps was that we weren\'t gassed or shot in the former. I do not recall a single act of compassion or mercy on the part of the Germans." Typical meals consisted of a bread slice and watery potato soup which, however, was still more substantial than what Soviet POWs or concentration camp inmates received. Another prisoner stated that "The German plan was to keep us alive, yet weakened enough that we wouldn\'t attempt escape."<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As Soviet ground forces approached some POW camps in early 1945, German guards <a href="/wiki/The_March_(1945)" title="The March (1945)">forced western Allied POWs to walk</a> long distances towards central Germany, often in extreme winter weather conditions.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>  It is estimated that, out of 257,000 POWs, about 80,000 were subject to such marches and up to 3,500 of them died as a result.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Italian_POWs">Italian POWs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Italian POWs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Operation_Achse" title="Operation Achse">Operation Achse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italian_military_internees" title="Italian military internees">Italian military internees</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Massacre_of_the_Acqui_Division" title="Massacre of the Acqui Division">Massacre of the Acqui Division</a></div>\n<p>In September 1943 after the Armistice, Italian officers and soldiers that in many places waited for clear superior orders, were arrested by Germans and Italian fascists and taken to German internment camps in Germany or Eastern Europe, where they were held for the duration of World War II. The International Red Cross could do nothing for them, as they were not regarded as POWs, but the prisoners held the status of "<a href="/wiki/Italian_military_internees" title="Italian military internees">military internees</a>". Treatment of the prisoners was generally poor. The author <a href="/wiki/Giovannino_Guareschi" title="Giovannino Guareschi">Giovannino Guareschi</a> was among those interned and wrote about this time in his life. The book was translated and published as "My Secret Diary". He wrote about the hungers of semi-starvation, the casual murder of individual prisoners by guards and how, when they were released (now from a German camp), they found a deserted German town filled with foodstuffs that they (with other released prisoners) ate.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>. It is estimated that of the 1,070,000 Italians taken prisoner by the Germans, around 40,000 died in detention and more than 13,000 lost their lives during the transportation from the Greek islands to the mainland.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Eastern_European_POWs">Eastern European POWs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Eastern European POWs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/German_mistreatment_of_Soviet_prisoners_of_war" title="German mistreatment of Soviet prisoners of war">German mistreatment of Soviet prisoners of war</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-B21845,_Sowjetische_Kriegsgefangene_im_Lager.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-B21845%2C_Sowjetische_Kriegsgefangene_im_Lager.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-B21845%2C_Sowjetische_Kriegsgefangene_im_Lager.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-B21845%2C_Sowjetische_Kriegsgefangene_im_Lager.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-B21845%2C_Sowjetische_Kriegsgefangene_im_Lager.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-B21845%2C_Sowjetische_Kriegsgefangene_im_Lager.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-B21845%2C_Sowjetische_Kriegsgefangene_im_Lager.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="532" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-B21845,_Sowjetische_Kriegsgefangene_im_Lager.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An improvised camp for Soviet POWs. Between June 1941 and January 1942, the Nazis killed an estimated 2.8 million Soviet prisoners of war, whom they viewed as "<a href="/wiki/Untermensch" title="Untermensch">subhuman</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Germany did not apply the same standard of treatment to non-western prisoners, especially many Polish and Soviet POWs who suffered harsh conditions and died in large numbers while in captivity.\n</p><p>Between 1941 and 1945 the Axis powers took about 5.7 million Soviet prisoners. About one million of them were released during the war, in that their status changed but they remained under German authority. A little over 500,000 either escaped or were liberated by the Red Army. Some 930,000 more were found alive in camps after the war. The remaining 3.3 million prisoners (57.5% of the total captured) died during their captivity.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Between the launching of <a href="/wiki/Operation_Barbarossa" title="Operation Barbarossa">Operation Barbarossa</a> in the summer of 1941 and the following spring, 2.8 million of the 3.2 million Soviet prisoners taken died while in German hands.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> According to Russian military historian General <a href="/wiki/Grigoriy_Krivosheyev" class="mw-redirect" title="Grigoriy Krivosheyev">Grigoriy Krivosheyev</a>, the Axis powers took 4.6 million Soviet prisoners, of whom 1.8 million were found alive in camps after the war and 318,770 were released by the Axis during the war and were then drafted into the Soviet armed forces again.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> By comparison, 8,348 Western Allied prisoners died in German camps during 1939\xe2\x80\x9345 (3.5% of the 232,000 total).<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_192-208,_KZ_Mauthausen,_Sowjetische_Kriegsgefangene.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Bundesarchiv_Bild_192-208%2C_KZ_Mauthausen%2C_Sowjetische_Kriegsgefangene.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_192-208%2C_KZ_Mauthausen%2C_Sowjetische_Kriegsgefangene.jpg" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Bundesarchiv_Bild_192-208%2C_KZ_Mauthausen%2C_Sowjetische_Kriegsgefangene.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_192-208%2C_KZ_Mauthausen%2C_Sowjetische_Kriegsgefangene.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Bundesarchiv_Bild_192-208%2C_KZ_Mauthausen%2C_Sowjetische_Kriegsgefangene.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_192-208%2C_KZ_Mauthausen%2C_Sowjetische_Kriegsgefangene.jpg 2x" data-file-width="779" data-file-height="494" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_192-208,_KZ_Mauthausen,_Sowjetische_Kriegsgefangene.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Naked Soviet prisoners of war in <a href="/wiki/Mauthausen_concentration_camp" class="mw-redirect" title="Mauthausen concentration camp">Mauthausen concentration camp</a>.</div></div></div><p> The Germans officially justified their policy on the grounds that the Soviet Union had not signed the Geneva Convention. Legally, however, under article 82 of the <a href="/wiki/Geneva_Convention_(1929)" title="Geneva Convention (1929)">Geneva Convention</a>, signatory countries had to give POWs of all signatory and non-signatory countries the rights assigned by the convention.<sup id="cite_ref-art%2E%2082_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-art._82-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Shortly after the German invasion in 1941, the USSR made Berlin an offer of a reciprocal adherence to the <a href="/wiki/Hague_Conventions_(1899_and_1907)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hague Conventions (1899 and 1907)">Hague Conventions</a>. Third Reich officials left the Soviet "note"  unanswered.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> In contrast, <a href="/wiki/Nikolai_Tolstoy" title="Nikolai Tolstoy">Nikolai Tolstoy</a> recounts that the German Government - as well as the <a href="/wiki/International_Red_Cross" class="mw-redirect" title="International Red Cross">International Red Cross</a> - made several efforts to regulate reciprocal treatment of prisoners until early 1942, but received no answers from the Soviet side.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Further, the Soviets took a harsh position towards captured Soviet soldiers, as they expected each soldier to <a href="/wiki/Order_No._270" title="Order No. 270">fight to the death</a>, and automatically excluded any prisoner from the "Russian community".<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Quotation needed from source to verify. (August 2017)">need quotation to verify</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Some Soviet POWs and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_worker" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern worker">forced labourers</a> whom the Germans had transported to <a href="/wiki/World_War_II#Casualties,_civilian_impact,_and_atrocities" title="World War II">Nazi Germany</a> were, on their return to the USSR, treated as traitors and sent to <a href="/wiki/Gulag" title="Gulag">gulag</a> prison-camps.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment_of_POWs_by_the_Soviet_Union">Treatment of POWs by the Soviet Union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Treatment of POWs by the Soviet Union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-E0406-0022-011,_Russland,_deutscher_Kriegsgefangener.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-E0406-0022-011%2C_Russland%2C_deutscher_Kriegsgefangener.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-E0406-0022-011%2C_Russland%2C_deutscher_Kriegsgefangener.jpg" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-E0406-0022-011%2C_Russland%2C_deutscher_Kriegsgefangener.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-E0406-0022-011%2C_Russland%2C_deutscher_Kriegsgefangener.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-E0406-0022-011%2C_Russland%2C_deutscher_Kriegsgefangener.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-E0406-0022-011%2C_Russland%2C_deutscher_Kriegsgefangener.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="517" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-E0406-0022-011,_Russland,_deutscher_Kriegsgefangener.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>German POW at <a href="/wiki/Stalingrad" class="mw-redirect" title="Stalingrad">Stalingrad</a></div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/POW_labor_in_the_Soviet_Union" title="POW labor in the Soviet Union">POW labor in the Soviet Union</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_prisoners_of_war_in_the_Soviet_Union" title="Japanese prisoners of war in the Soviet Union">Japanese prisoners of war in the Soviet Union</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italian_prisoners_of_war_in_the_Soviet_Union" title="Italian prisoners of war in the Soviet Union">Italian prisoners of war in the Soviet Union</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romanian_POW_in_the_Soviet_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Romanian POW in the Soviet Union">Romanian POW in the Soviet Union</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polish_prisoners_of_war_in_the_Soviet_Union_(after_1939)" class="mw-redirect" title="Polish prisoners of war in the Soviet Union (after 1939)">Polish prisoners of war in the Soviet Union (after 1939)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finnish_prisoners_of_war_in_the_Soviet_Union" title="Finnish prisoners of war in the Soviet Union">Finnish prisoners of war in the Soviet Union</a>, <a href="/wiki/German_prisoners_of_war_in_the_Soviet_Union" title="German prisoners of war in the Soviet Union">German prisoners of war in the Soviet Union</a>, <a href="/wiki/Katyn_massacre" title="Katyn massacre">Katyn massacre</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gulag" title="Gulag">Gulag</a></div>\n<h4><span id="Germans.2C_Romanians.2C_Italians.2C_Hungarians.2C_Finns"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Germans,_Romanians,_Italians,_Hungarians,_Finns">Germans, Romanians, Italians, Hungarians, Finns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Germans, Romanians, Italians, Hungarians, Finns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>According to some sources, the Soviets captured 3.5 million <a href="/wiki/Axis_powers_of_World_War_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Axis powers of World War II">Axis</a> servicemen (excluding Japanese) of which more than a million died.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> One specific example is that of the German POWs after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Stalingrad" title="Battle of Stalingrad">Battle of Stalingrad</a>, where the Soviets captured 91,000 German troops in total (completely exhausted, starving and sick) of whom only 5,000 survived the captivity.\n</p><p>German soldiers were kept as forced labour for many years after the war. The last German POWs like <a href="/wiki/Erich_Hartmann" title="Erich Hartmann">Erich Hartmann</a>, the highest-scoring <a href="/wiki/Flying_ace" title="Flying ace">fighter ace</a> in the history of <a href="/wiki/Aerial_warfare" title="Aerial warfare">aerial warfare</a>, who had been declared guilty of <a href="/wiki/War_crime" title="War crime">war crimes</a> but without <a href="/wiki/Due_process" title="Due process">due process</a>, were not released by the Soviets until 1955, three years after Stalin died.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Polish">Polish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Polish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Katy%C5%84,_ekshumacja_ofiar.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Katy%C5%84%2C_ekshumacja_ofiar.jpg/220px-Katy%C5%84%2C_ekshumacja_ofiar.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Katy%C5%84%2C_ekshumacja_ofiar.jpg/330px-Katy%C5%84%2C_ekshumacja_ofiar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Katy%C5%84%2C_ekshumacja_ofiar.jpg/440px-Katy%C5%84%2C_ekshumacja_ofiar.jpg 2x" data-file-width="616" data-file-height="440" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Katy%C5%84,_ekshumacja_ofiar.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Katyn 1943 exhumation. Photo by <a href="/wiki/International_Red_Cross" class="mw-redirect" title="International Red Cross">International Red Cross</a> delegation.</div></div></div>\n<p>As a result of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_invasion_of_Poland" title="Soviet invasion of Poland">Soviet invasion of Poland</a> in 1939, hundreds of thousands of Polish soldiers became <a href="/wiki/Polish_prisoners_of_war_in_the_Soviet_Union_(after_1939)" class="mw-redirect" title="Polish prisoners of war in the Soviet Union (after 1939)">prisoners of war in the Soviet Union</a>. Thousands of them were executed; over 20,000 Polish military personnel and civilians perished in the <a href="/wiki/Katyn_massacre" title="Katyn massacre">Katyn massacre</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Fischer_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Out of <a href="/wiki/W%C5%82adys%C5%82aw_Anders" title="W\xc5\x82adys\xc5\x82aw Anders">Anders</a>\' 80,000 evacuees from Soviet Union gathered in the United Kingdom only 310 volunteered to return to Poland in 1947.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Out of the 230,000 Polish prisoners of war taken by the Soviet army, only 82,000 survived.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Japanese">Japanese</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Japanese">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>With the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_invasion_of_Manchuria" title="Soviet invasion of Manchuria">Soviet invasion of Manchuria</a>, in 1945, Japanese soldiers became prisoners in the Soviet Union, where they, just as other Axis POWs, had to work.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Americans">Americans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Americans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>There were stories during the Cold War to the effect that 23,000 Americans who had been held in German POW camps were seized by the Soviets and never repatriated. This myth had been perpetuated after the release of people like <a href="/wiki/John_H._Noble" title="John H. Noble">John H. Noble</a>. Careful scholarly studies have demonstrated this is a myth based on a misinterpretation of a telegram that was talking about Soviet prisoners held in Italy.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment_of_POWs_by_the_Western_Allies">Treatment of POWs by the Western Allies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Treatment of POWs by the Western Allies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_World_War_II_POW_camps" class="mw-redirect" title="List of World War II POW camps">List of World War II POW camps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Allied_war_crimes_during_World_War_II" title="Allied war crimes during World War II">Allied war crimes during World War II</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Operation_Keelhaul" title="Operation Keelhaul">Operation Keelhaul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Forced_labor_of_Germans_after_World_War_II" title="Forced labor of Germans after World War II">Forced labor of Germans after World War II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_prisoners_of_war_in_World_War_II" title="Japanese prisoners of war in World War II">Japanese prisoners of war in World War II</a>, and <a href="/wiki/German_prisoners_of_war_in_the_United_States" title="German prisoners of war in the United States">German prisoners of war in the United States</a></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Germans">Germans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Germans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Remagen_enclosure.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Remagen_enclosure.jpg/220px-Remagen_enclosure.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Remagen_enclosure.jpg/330px-Remagen_enclosure.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Remagen_enclosure.jpg/440px-Remagen_enclosure.jpg 2x" data-file-width="614" data-file-height="437" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Remagen_enclosure.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Remagen" title="Remagen">Remagen</a> open-field <i><a href="/wiki/Rheinwiesenlager" title="Rheinwiesenlager">Rheinwiesenlager</a></i></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gefangenen-Meldung_Vorderseite.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Gefangenen-Meldung_Vorderseite.jpg/220px-Gefangenen-Meldung_Vorderseite.jpg" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Gefangenen-Meldung_Vorderseite.jpg/330px-Gefangenen-Meldung_Vorderseite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Gefangenen-Meldung_Vorderseite.jpg/440px-Gefangenen-Meldung_Vorderseite.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="578" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gefangenen-Meldung_Vorderseite.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>US Army: Card of capture for German POWs \xe2\x80\x93 front</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gefangenen-Meldung_Rueckseite.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Gefangenen-Meldung_Rueckseite.jpg/220px-Gefangenen-Meldung_Rueckseite.jpg" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Gefangenen-Meldung_Rueckseite.jpg/330px-Gefangenen-Meldung_Rueckseite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Gefangenen-Meldung_Rueckseite.jpg/440px-Gefangenen-Meldung_Rueckseite.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="578" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gefangenen-Meldung_Rueckseite.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The reverse of above card</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:EB-Kriegsgefangenen-Entlassungsausweis.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/EB-Kriegsgefangenen-Entlassungsausweis.jpg/220px-EB-Kriegsgefangenen-Entlassungsausweis.jpg" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/EB-Kriegsgefangenen-Entlassungsausweis.jpg/330px-EB-Kriegsgefangenen-Entlassungsausweis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/EB-Kriegsgefangenen-Entlassungsausweis.jpg/440px-EB-Kriegsgefangenen-Entlassungsausweis.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3016" data-file-height="1988" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:EB-Kriegsgefangenen-Entlassungsausweis.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Certificate of Discharge<br />of a German General<br />(Front- and Backside)</div></div></div>\n<p>During the war, the armies of Western Allied nations such as Australia, Canada, the UK and the US<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> were ordered to treat <a href="/wiki/Axis_powers_of_World_War_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Axis powers of World War II">Axis</a> prisoners strictly in accordance with the <a href="/wiki/Geneva_Convention_(1929)" title="Geneva Convention (1929)">Geneva Convention</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Some breaches of the Convention took place, however. According to <a href="/wiki/Stephen_E._Ambrose" title="Stephen E. Ambrose">Stephen E. Ambrose</a>, of the roughly 1,000 US combat veterans that he had interviewed, only one admitted to shooting a prisoner, saying that he "felt remorse, but would do it again". However, one-third told him they had seen US troops kill German prisoners.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Towards the end of the war in Europe, as large numbers of Axis soldiers surrendered, the US created the designation of <a href="/wiki/Disarmed_Enemy_Forces" title="Disarmed Enemy Forces">Disarmed Enemy Forces</a> (DEF) so as not to treat prisoners as POWs. A lot of these soldiers were kept in open fields in makeshift camps in the Rhine valley (<i><a href="/wiki/Rheinwiesenlager" title="Rheinwiesenlager">Rheinwiesenlager</a></i>). Controversy has arisen about how Eisenhower managed these prisoners<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> (see <i><a href="/wiki/Other_Losses" title="Other Losses">Other Losses</a></i>).\n</p><p>After the surrender of Germany in May 1945, the POW status of the German prisoners was in many cases maintained, and they were for several years used as forced labour in countries such as the UK and France. Many died when forced to clear minefields in Norway, France etc.; "by September 1945 it was estimated by the French authorities that two thousand prisoners were being maimed and killed each month in accidents"<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1946, the UK had more than 400,000 German prisoners, many had been transferred from POW camps in the US and Canada. Many of these were for over three years after the German surrender used as forced labour, as a form of "reparations".<sup id="cite_ref-Noam_Chomsky_1979_pp._35_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Noam_Chomsky_1979_pp._35-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> A public debate ensued in the UK, where words such as "forced labour", "slaves", "slave labour" were increasingly used in the media and in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-German_migrants_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-German_migrants-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> In 1947 the Ministry of Agriculture argued against repatriation of working German prisoners, since by then they made up 25 percent of the land workforce, and they wanted to use them also in 1948.<sup id="cite_ref-German_migrants_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-German_migrants-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The "<a href="/wiki/London_Cage" title="London Cage">London Cage</a>", an <a href="/wiki/MI19" title="MI19">MI19</a> prisoner of war facility in the UK used for interrogating prisoners before they were sent to prison camps during and immediately after World War II, was subject to allegations of torture.<sup id="cite_ref-Secrets_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Secrets-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the German surrender, the International Red Cross was prohibited from providing aid such as food or visiting prisoner camps in Germany. However, after making approaches to the Allies in the autumn of 1945 it was allowed to investigate the camps in the British and French occupation zones of Germany, as well as to provide relief to the prisoners held there.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2005_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2005-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> On 4 February 1946, the Red Cross was permitted to visit and assist prisoners also in the US occupation zone of Germany, although only with very small quantities of food. "During their visits, the delegates observed that German prisoners of war were often detained in appalling conditions. They drew the attention of the authorities to this fact, and gradually succeeded in getting some improvements made".<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2005_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2005-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Allies also shipped POWs between them, with for example 6,000 German officers transferred from Western Allied camps to the <a href="/wiki/Sachsenhausen_concentration_camp" title="Sachsenhausen concentration camp">Sachsenhausen concentration camp</a> that now was under Soviet Union administration.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> The US also shipped 740,000 German POWs as forced labourers to France from where newspaper reports told of very bad treatment. Judge <a href="/wiki/Robert_H._Jackson" title="Robert H. Jackson">Robert H. Jackson</a>, Chief US prosecutor in the <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_trials" title="Nuremberg trials">Nuremberg trials</a>, in October 1945 told US President <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a> that the Allies themselves:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>have done or are doing some of the very things we are prosecuting the Germans for. The French are so violating the Geneva Convention in the treatment of prisoners of war that our command is taking back prisoners sent to them. We are prosecuting plunder and our Allies are practicing it.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hungarians">Hungarians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Hungarians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Hungarians became POWs of the Western Allies. Some of these were, like Germans, used as forced labour in France after the cessation of hostilities.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\nAfter the war the POWs were handed over to the Soviets, and after the POWs were transported to the <a href="/wiki/USSR" class="mw-redirect" title="USSR">USSR</a> for <a href="/wiki/Unfree_labour" title="Unfree labour">forced labour</a>. It is called even today in <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a> <a href="/wiki/Malenkij_robot" class="mw-redirect" title="Malenkij robot">malenkij robot</a>\xe2\x80\x94little work. <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A1s_Toma" title="Andr\xc3\xa1s Toma">Andr\xc3\xa1s Toma</a>, a Hungarian soldier taken prisoner by the Red Army in 1944, was discovered in a Russian psychiatric hospital in 2000. He was probably the last prisoner of war from World War II to be repatriated.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Japanese_2">Japanese</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Japanese">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:OkinawaJapanesePOW.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/OkinawaJapanesePOW.jpg/220px-OkinawaJapanesePOW.jpg" width="220" height="321" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/OkinawaJapanesePOW.jpg/330px-OkinawaJapanesePOW.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/OkinawaJapanesePOW.jpg/440px-OkinawaJapanesePOW.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="2985" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:OkinawaJapanesePOW.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A group of Japanese captured during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Okinawa" title="Battle of Okinawa">Battle of Okinawa</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Although thousands of Japanese were taken prisoner, most fought until they were killed or committed suicide. Of the 22,000 Japanese soldiers present at the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Iwo_Jima" title="Battle of Iwo Jima">Battle of Iwo Jima</a>, over 20,000 were killed and only 216 were taken prisoner.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Of the 30,000 Japanese troops that defended <a href="/wiki/Saipan" title="Saipan">Saipan</a>, fewer than 1,000 remained alive at battle\'s end.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Japanese prisoners sent to camps fared well; however, some Japanese were killed when trying to surrender or were massacred<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> just after they had surrendered (see <a href="/wiki/Allied_war_crimes_during_World_War_II#The_Pacific" title="Allied war crimes during World War II">Allied war crimes during World War II in the Pacific</a>). In some instances, Japanese prisoners were tortured by a variety of methods.<sup id="cite_ref-cnn19969623_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnn19969623-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> A method of torture used by the Chinese <a href="/wiki/National_Revolutionary_Army" title="National Revolutionary Army">National Revolutionary Army</a> (NRA) included suspending the prisoner by the neck in a wooden cage until they died.<sup id="cite_ref-cnn19969623_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnn19969623-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> In very rare cases, some were beheaded by sword, and a severed head was once used as a football by Chinese National Revolutionary Army (NRA) soldiers.<sup id="cite_ref-cnn19969623_102-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnn19969623-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the war, many Japanese were kept on as <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Surrendered_Personnel" title="Japanese Surrendered Personnel">Japanese Surrendered Personnel</a> until mid-1947 and used as forced labour doing menial tasks, while 35,000 were kept on in arms within their wartime military organisation and under their own officers and used in combat alongside British troops seeking to suppress the independence movements in the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_Indies" title="Dutch East Indies">Dutch East Indies</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_Indochina" title="French Indochina">French Indochina</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Italians">Italians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Italians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In 1943, Italy overthrew Mussolini and became a co-belligerent with the Allies. This did not mean any change in status for Italian POWs however, since due to the labour shortages in the UK, Australia and the USA, they were retained as POWs there.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cossacks">Cossacks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Cossacks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>On 11 February 1945, at the conclusion of the <a href="/wiki/Yalta_Conference" title="Yalta Conference">Yalta Conference</a>, the United States and the United Kingdom signed a Repatriation Agreement with the USSR.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> The interpretation of this Agreement resulted in the forcible repatriation of all Soviets (<a href="/wiki/Operation_Keelhaul" title="Operation Keelhaul">Operation Keelhaul</a>) regardless of their wishes. The forced repatriation operations took place in 1945\xe2\x80\x931947.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Transfers_between_the_Allies">Transfers between the Allies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Transfers between the Allies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The United States handed over 740,000 German prisoners to France, a signatory of the Geneva Convention. The Soviet Union had not signed the Geneva Convention. According to Edward Peterson, the U.S. chose to hand over several hundred thousand German prisoners to the Soviet Union in May 1945 as a "gesture of friendship".<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> U.S. forces also refused to accept the surrender of German troops attempting to surrender to them in <a href="/wiki/Saxony" title="Saxony">Saxony</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemia</a>, and handed them over to the Soviet Union instead.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> It is also known that 6000 of the German officers who were sent from camps in the West to the Soviets were subsequently imprisoned in the <a href="/wiki/Sachsenhausen_concentration_camp" title="Sachsenhausen concentration camp">Sachsenhausen concentration camp</a>, which at the time was one of the <a href="/wiki/NKVD_special_camps" class="mw-redirect" title="NKVD special camps">NKVD special camp</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post_World_War_II">Post World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Post World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Americanexecuted1950korea.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Americanexecuted1950korea.jpg/220px-Americanexecuted1950korea.jpg" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Americanexecuted1950korea.jpg/330px-Americanexecuted1950korea.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Americanexecuted1950korea.jpg/440px-Americanexecuted1950korea.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2923" data-file-height="2211" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Americanexecuted1950korea.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A U.S. Army POW of the <a href="/wiki/21st_Infantry_Regiment_(United_States)" title="21st Infantry Regiment (United States)">21st Infantry Regiment</a> bound and killed by North Koreans during the Korean War.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USVietPeace.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/USVietPeace.JPG/220px-USVietPeace.JPG" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/USVietPeace.JPG/330px-USVietPeace.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/USVietPeace.JPG/440px-USVietPeace.JPG 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="421" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USVietPeace.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An American POW being released by his North Vietnamese and Viet Cong captors in February 1973.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hanoi-taxi-march1973.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Hanoi-taxi-march1973.jpg/220px-Hanoi-taxi-march1973.jpg" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Hanoi-taxi-march1973.jpg/330px-Hanoi-taxi-march1973.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Hanoi-taxi-march1973.jpg/440px-Hanoi-taxi-march1973.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="423" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hanoi-taxi-march1973.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Recently released American POWs from North Vietnamese prison camps in 1973.</div></div></div>\n<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>, the North Koreans developed reputation for severely mistreating prisoners of war (see <a href="/wiki/Korean_War#Prisoners_of_war" title="Korean War">Crimes against POWs</a>). Their POWs were housed in three camps, according to their potential usefulness to the North Korean army. Peace camps and reform camps were for POWs that were either sympathetic to the cause or who had valued skills that could be useful in the army and thus these enemy soldiers were indoctrinated and sometimes conscripted into the North Korean army. The regular prisoners of war were usually very poorly treated. POWs in peace camps were reportedly treated with more consideration.<sup id="cite_ref-Three_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Three-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1952, the 1952 Inter-Camp P.O.W. Olympics were held during 15 and 27 November 1952, in Pyuktong, <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>. The Chinese hoped to gain worldwide publicity and while some prisoners refused to participate some 500 P.O.W.s of eleven nationalities took part.<sup id="cite_ref-Adams,_2007,_p._62_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adams,_2007,_p._62-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> They were representative of all the prison camps in North Korea and competed in: football, baseball, softball, basketball, volleyball, track and field, soccer, gymnastics, and <a href="/wiki/Boxing" title="Boxing">boxing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Adams,_2007,_p._62_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adams,_2007,_p._62-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> For the P.O.W.s this was also an opportunity to meet with friends from other camps. The prisoners had their own photographers, announcers, even reporters, who after each day\'s competition published a newspaper, the "Olympic Roundup".<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Of about 16,500 French soldiers who fought at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dien_Bien_Phu" title="Battle of Dien Bien Phu">Battle of Dien Bien Phu</a> in <a href="/wiki/French_Indochina" title="French Indochina">French Indochina</a>, more than 3,000 were killed in battle, while almost all of the 11,721 men taken prisoner died in the hands of the <a href="/wiki/Viet_Minh" class="mw-redirect" title="Viet Minh">Viet Minh</a> on death marches to distant POW camps, and in those camps in the last three months of the war.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Vietcong" class="mw-redirect" title="Vietcong">Vietcong</a> and the <a href="/wiki/North_Vietnamese_Army" class="mw-redirect" title="North Vietnamese Army">North Vietnamese Army</a> captured many <a href="/wiki/United_States_Armed_Forces" title="United States Armed Forces">United States service members</a> as prisoners of war during the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>, who suffered from mistreatment and torture during the war. Some American prisoners were held in the prison called the <a href="/wiki/Hanoi_Hilton" class="mw-redirect" title="Hanoi Hilton">Hanoi Hilton</a>.\n</p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/U.S._prisoners_of_war_during_the_Vietnam_War" title="U.S. prisoners of war during the Vietnam War">U.S. prisoners of war during the Vietnam War</a></div>\n<p>Communist Vietnamese held in custody by <a href="/wiki/South_Vietnam" title="South Vietnam">South Vietnamese</a> and American forces <a href="/wiki/C%C3%B4n_S%C6%A1n_Island#&quot;Tiger_cages&quot;" title="C\xc3\xb4n S\xc6\xa1n Island">were also tortured</a> and badly treated.<sup id="cite_ref-south-viet_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-south-viet-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> After the war, millions of South Vietnamese servicemen and government workers were sent to <a href="/wiki/Reeducation_camp" title="Reeducation camp">"re-education" camps</a> where many perished.\n</p><p>Like in previous conflicts, there has been speculation without evidence that there were a handful of American pilots captured by the North Koreans and the North Vietnamese who were transferred to the Soviet Union and were never repatriated.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Regardless of regulations determining treatment to prisoners, violations of their rights continue to be reported. Many cases of POW massacres have been reported in recent times, including <a href="/wiki/October_13_massacre" title="October 13 massacre">October 13 massacre</a> in Lebanon by Syrian forces and <a href="/wiki/Massacre_of_police_officers_in_Eastern_Sri_Lanka_in_June_1990" class="mw-redirect" title="Massacre of police officers in Eastern Sri Lanka in June 1990">June 1990 massacre</a> in Sri Lanka.\n</p><p>In 1982, during the <a href="/wiki/Falklands_War" title="Falklands War">Falklands War</a>, prisoners were well treated in general by both parties of the conflict, with military commanders dispatching \'enemy\' prisoners back to their homelands in record time.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1991, during the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Persian Gulf War</a>, American, British, Italian, and Kuwaiti POWs (mostly crew members of downed aircraft and special forces) were tortured by the Iraqi secret police. An American military doctor, <a href="/wiki/Rhonda_Cornum" title="Rhonda Cornum">Major Rhonda Cornum</a>, a 37-year-old flight surgeon captured when her Blackhawk UH-60 was shot down, was also subjected to sexual abuse.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the 1990s <a href="/wiki/Yugoslav_Wars" title="Yugoslav Wars">Yugoslav Wars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serb" class="mw-redirect" title="Serb">Serb</a> paramilitary forces supported by JNA forces killed POWs at <a href="/wiki/Vukovar_massacre" title="Vukovar massacre">Vukovar</a> and <a href="/wiki/%C5%A0kabrnja_massacre" title="\xc5\xa0kabrnja massacre">\xc5\xa0karbrnja</a> while <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_Serb" class="mw-redirect" title="Bosnian Serb">Bosnian Serb</a> forces killed POWs at <a href="/wiki/Srebrenica_massacre" title="Srebrenica massacre">Srebrenica</a>.\n</p><p>In 2001, there were reports concerning two POWs that India had taken during the <a href="/wiki/Sino-Indian_War" title="Sino-Indian War">Sino-Indian War</a>, Yang Chen and Shih Liang. The two were imprisoned as spies for three years before being interned in a <a href="/wiki/Mental_asylum" class="mw-redirect" title="Mental asylum">mental asylum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ranchi" title="Ranchi">Ranchi</a>, where they spent the next 38 years under a special prisoner status.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><br /> The last prisoners of <a href="/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Iraq_War" title="Iran\xe2\x80\x93Iraq War">Iran\xe2\x80\x93Iraq War</a> (1980\xe2\x80\x931988) were exchanged in 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Numbers_of_POWs">Numbers of POWs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prisoner_of_war&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Numbers of POWs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>This article is a list of nations with the highest number of POWs since the start of World War II, listed in descending order. These are also the highest numbers in any war since the <a href="/wiki/Geneva_Convention_(1929)" title="Geneva Convention (1929)">Convention Relative to the Treatment of Prisoners of War</a> entered into force on 19 June 1931. The USSR had not signed the Geneva convention.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" When this dolorous decree, and pitifull proclamation was pronounced, pitie it was to see how some Frenchmen were suddenlie sticked with daggers, some were brained with pollaxes, some slaine with malls, others had their throats cut, and some their bellies panched, so that in effect, having respect to the great number, few prisoners were saved."&#160;: <a href="/wiki/Raphael_Holinshed" title="Raphael Holinshed">Raphael Holinshed</a>\'s <i>Chronicles of England, Scotland and Ireland</i>, quoted by Andrew Gurr in his introduction to <cite class="citation book">Shakespeare, William; Gurr, Andrew (2005). <i>King Henry V</i>.
